type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"BreadcrumbList","itemListElement":[{"@type":"ListItem","item":{"@id":"https://www.scmp.com/news","name":"News"},"position":1},{"@type":"ListItem","item":{"@id":"https://www.scmp.com/news/china","name":"China"},"position":2},{"@type":"ListItem","item":{"@id":"https://www.scmp.com/news/china/science","name":"Science"},"position":3}]}</script><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@id":"https://www.scmp.com/news/china/science/article/3241483/chinas-best-defence-poultry-vaccine-bird-flu-spreads-europe-africa","@type":"WebPage"}</script><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":["NewsArticle","ReportageNewsArticle"],"alternativeHeadline":"Poultry vaccine China’s ‘best defence’ as bird flu spreads from Europe, Africa","articleBody":"The deadly virus behind bird flu has become better adapted to wild birds and is shifting from China to Europe and northern Africa, in what a study led by the University of Hong Kong (HKU) has called “an environmental disaster”.\nThe team, which included researchers in Australia, Egypt, France, the United Arab Emirates and the United States, found that recent outbreaks suggested the epicentre for the H5N1 virus had extended beyond Asia.\nIn a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature, the researchers said the virus had also become more persistent in wild bird populations, driving the evolution and spread of new strains, putting marine and land mammals at risk across the world.\n“Since November 2021, this H5N1 virus has caused unprecedented outbreaks in diverse wild bird species across five continents and a significant rise in incidental infections in wild carnivores, mink farms and marine mammals,” they said.\nLead author Vijaykrishna Dhanasekaran, who heads the HKU pathogen evolution lab, urged China to guard against strains of the virus arriving from Europe by keeping up its vaccination programme against bird flu infections among the country’s flocks.\nWhy sick minks are reigniting worries about bird flu spreading to humans\n“Especially since the emergence of H7N9 in 2013 … the control in China has become really good with mass application of H5 and H7 vaccines. That is one of the reasons the recent resurgence was not in China,” he said.\n“They need to sustain it because now the viruses are coming back from Europe. [China should] continue the vaccination system so that we can keep eliminating the virus.”\nH5N1 first emerged in China in 1996 and was the first virus to establish sustained transmission in domestic poultry. After years of being largely confined to Asian poultry networks, recent outbreaks have emerged further afield.\nOn the rare occasions the virus appeared in Europe and Africa, it was carried there by wild birds but would die out after a few months because H5N1 was not as adaptive to wild populations.\n“However, in the past two years we have seen extensive outbreaks – they’re doubling,” said Dhanasekaran, who is also an associate professor with the HKU school of public health.\n“It’s an environmental disaster in terms of the number of wild birds that have been infected. The virus is also spreading to new regions via these migratory flyways.”\n‘Extremely rare’ case of bird flu in human detected in England\nIn September, three adult harbour seals in Puget Sound, on the northwestern coast of Washington state, tested positive for the H5N1 virus, according to the US National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.\nIt was the first time the highly pathogenic bird flu was reported in marine mammals on the US West Coast.\nLast month, concerns were raised that penguins and other local species could be at risk after H5N1 was detected in the Antarctic region for the first time.\nThe virus was probably carried to Antarctica by migratory birds returning from South America, where there has been a high number of bird flu cases, according to the British Antarctic Survey.\nBird flu detected in Antarctica region for first time\nDhanasekaran said the H7 and H9 virus groups mainly infected chickens, while the biggest difficulty in controlling the H5 viruses was that they affected aquatic and terrestrial poultry differently.\n“When [H5] is infecting … poultry, it can kill chickens but it does not kill ducks. It is naturally adapted to ducks so when it is circulating in the live bird markets, we can never be sure until it infects chickens and they start dying,” he said.\nThe paper warned that the scale of H5 outbreaks in wild birds had been escalating beyond Asia since 2014. The researchers said recent incidences in African and European bird populations suggested the epicentre had also extended beyond the Asian region.\n“It is necessary to enhance global surveillance and improve multifaceted mitigation strategies for outbreak prevention and response,” the scientists said.\nBird flu outbreak: 500,000 chickens die in Burkina Faso\nThe World Health Organization recorded 1,566 cases of human infection and at least 613 deaths from the H7N9 virus between 2013 and 2018. China remained the epicentre of the virus until 2016.\nA 2021 study by the State Key Laboratory of Veterinary Biotechnology in Harbin, northern China, identified five waves of human infection caused by the H7N9 bird flu virus, and confirmed that it caused only mild infections in ducks while being highly lethal to chickens.\nThe Harbin researchers noted that “further human cases have been successfully prevented since September 2017 through the use of an H7N9 vaccine in poultry” but the virus had not been eradicated from poultry.\nThey found that the H7N9 viruses isolated in 2019 were antigenically different from the vaccine strain used to control the virus in poultry. “Replication of these viruses cannot therefore be completely prevented in vaccinated chickens.”\n\n\nMeanwhile in Britain, scientists proposed gene editing as a possible way to breed chickens that are partially resistant to bird flu, in a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature Communications in October.\nThe team said it was able to restrict the virus from infecting chickens by altering a small section of their DNA. The researchers found fully grown chickens were resistant to a very low dose of the flu from infected birds, but not at doses 1,000 times higher.\nCo-author Mike McGrew, personal chair of avian reproductive technologies at the University of Edinburgh’s Roslin Institute, said gene editing “promises a new way to make permanent changes in the disease resistance of an animal”.\n“This can be passed down through all the gene-edited animals to all the offspring so it would protect the poultry and reduce risk to the poultry farmers and wild birds,” he said.\nBird flu kills 11-year-old girl in Cambodia\nWendy Barclay, head of the department of infectious diseases at Imperial College London and the study’s other author, said her group discovered a gene called ANP32 which was “absolutely essential” to supporting the virus when it was inside the cell.\n“If you could prevent the protein from being used [to help viruses replicate] by gene editing, the virus would not be able to replicate. This strategy could be used not just for H5N1 bird flu, but for any of the strains, because it is fundamental to the way that the virus works,” she said.\nThe British scientists monitored the gene-edited birds for more than two years and said they showed no adverse effects on health or egg-laying productivity.\nThey cautioned that further study would be needed to ensure animal health, and that multiple edits of the gene family might be needed to eliminate the possibility of viral evolution.\n","articleSection":"Science","author":{"@type":"Person","name":"Holly Chik","sameAs":"https://www.scmp.com/author/holly-chik"},"dateCreated":"2023-11-14T15:45:10+08:00","dateModified":"2023-11-19T16:39:13+08:00","datePublished":"2023-11-19T16:00:15+08:00","description":"Epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds in ‘environmental disaster’, International research team led by University of Hong Kong class="js-reading-0-percent-completion-tracker css-19icq8a ekyy9du0" data-qa="useTrackViewedPercentageRender-trackViewedPercentageRenders-TrackPercentageBlock"></div><div datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1xdhyk6 ec74h0k0">The deadly virus behind <a target="_self" class="e1yy41x40 ef9u0v01 css-1ankfgb e1llv60l0" href="https://www.scmp.com/topics/bird-flu-viruses-H5N1-H3N8?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 ef9u0v00">bird flu</span></a> has become better adapted to wild birds and is shifting from China to Europe and northern Africa, in what a study led by the <a target="_self" class="e1yy41x40 ef9u0v01 css-1ankfgb e1llv60l0" href="https://www.scmp.com/news/hong-kong/education/article/3240815/university-hong-kong-unseats-singapores-top-varsity-influential-asia-ranking-peking-university-holds?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 ef9u0v00">University of Hong Kong</span></a> (HKU) has called “an environmental disaster”.</div><div data-qa="InlineAdSlot-Container" class=" css-zl1inp e11v3ui14"><div class="e11v3ui10 e11v3ui13 css-y2bwcc e1flwkbl0" data-qa="AdSlot-Container"><div data-qa="AdSlot-Label" class="css-1t8iakn e1flwkbl3">Advertisement</div><div data-qa="AdSlot-AdSlotContainer" class="css-0 e1flwkbl2"></div></div></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1c6uqr6 ec74h0k1">The team, which included researchers in Australia, Egypt, France, the United Arab Emirates and the United States, found that recent outbreaks suggested the epicentre for the H5N1 virus had extended beyond Asia.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1c6uqr6 ec74h0k1">In a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature, the researchers said the virus had also become more persistent in wild bird populations, driving the evolution and spread of new strains, putting marine and land mammals at risk across the world.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1c6uqr6 ec74h0k1">“Since November 2021, this H5N1 virus has caused unprecedented outbreaks in diverse wild bird species across five continents and a significant rise in incidental infections in wild carnivores, mink farms and marine mammals,” they said.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1c6uqr6 ec74h0k1">Lead author Vijaykrishna Dhanasekaran, who heads the HKU pathogen evolution lab, urged China to guard against strains of the virus arriving from Europe by keeping up its vaccination programme against bird flu infections among the country’s flocks.</p><h3 type="h3" data-qa="Component-Component" class="eh0yk8h0 css-17x7qa9 e1pqa4re1"></h3><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e8zc9q40 css-1c6uqr6 ec74h0k1">“Especially since the emergence of H7N9 in 2013 … the control in China has become really good with mass application of H5 and H7 vaccines. That is one of the reasons the recent resurgence was not in China,” he said.</p><div class="js-reading-20-percent-completion-tracker css-19icq8a ekyy9du0" Shutterstock","url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg","isSlideshow":true,"sizeLandscape250x99":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/landscape_250_99/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=ICjW8LwA\u0026v=1699947913"},"size1280x720":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/wide_landscape/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=ihFUIO9q\u0026v=1699947913"},"size1200x800":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/1200x800/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=RGRvOXd9\u0026v=1699947913"},"size1020x680":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/1020x680/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=IaFU7-sZ\u0026v=1699947913"},"size768x768":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/768x768/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=oh0JVwYL\u0026v=1699947913"},"landscape250x99":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/landscape_250_99/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=ICjW8LwA\u0026v=1699947913"},"size540x360":{"url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/styles/540x360/public/d8/images/canvas/2023/11/14/dadfb3c3-3526-4f46-bb7d-bca02d8b8a76_28820fa6.jpg?itok=MLLzidtB\u0026v=1699947913"},"height":2730,"width":4095}],"disableOffPlatformContent":[],"published":true,"sponsorType":null,"publishedDate":1700380815000,"updatedDate":1700383153000,"summary":{"text":"Epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds in ‘environmental disaster’, International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds.\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"Epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds in ‘environmental disaster’, International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds."}]}]},"subHeadline":{"text":"International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds\nWith infections recorded across 5 continents, including Antarctica, penguins and marine and land-based mammals are ‘at risk’\n","json":[{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"With infections recorded across 5 continents, including Antarctica, penguins and marine and land-based mammals are ‘at risk’"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"}]}]},"body":{"text":"The deadly virus behind bird flu has become better adapted to wild birds and is shifting from China to Europe and northern Africa, in what a study led by the University of Hong Kong (HKU) has called “an environmental disaster”.\nThe team, which included researchers in Australia, Egypt, France, the United Arab Emirates and the United States, found that recent outbreaks suggested the epicentre for the H5N1 virus had extended beyond Asia.\nIn a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature, the researchers said the virus had also become more persistent in wild bird populations, driving the evolution and spread of new strains, putting marine and land mammals at risk across the world.\n“Since November 2021, this H5N1 virus has caused unprecedented outbreaks in diverse wild bird species across five continents and a significant rise in incidental infections in wild carnivores, mink farms and marine mammals,” they said.\nLead author Vijaykrishna Dhanasekaran, who heads the HKU pathogen evolution lab, urged China to guard against strains of the virus arriving from Europe by keeping up its vaccination programme against bird flu infections among the country’s flocks.\nWhy sick minks are reigniting worries about bird flu spreading to humans\n“Especially since the emergence of H7N9 in 2013 … the control in China has become really good with mass application of H5 and H7 vaccines. That is one of the reasons the recent resurgence was not in China,” he said.\n“They need to sustain it because now the viruses are coming back from Europe. [China should] continue the vaccination system so that we can keep eliminating the virus.”\nH5N1 first emerged in China in 1996 and was the first virus to establish sustained transmission in domestic poultry. After years of being largely confined to Asian poultry networks, recent outbreaks have emerged further afield.\nOn the rare occasions the virus appeared in Europe and Africa, it was carried there by wild birds but would die out after a few months because H5N1 was not as adaptive to wild populations.\n“However, in the past two years we have seen extensive outbreaks – they’re doubling,” said Dhanasekaran, who is also an associate professor with the HKU school of public health.\n“It’s an environmental disaster in terms of the number of wild birds that have been infected. The virus is also spreading to new regions via these migratory flyways.”\n‘Extremely rare’ case of bird flu in human detected in England\nIn September, three adult harbour seals in Puget Sound, on the northwestern coast of Washington state, tested positive for the H5N1 virus, according to the US National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.\nIt was the first time the highly pathogenic bird flu was reported in marine mammals on the US West Coast.\nLast month, concerns were raised that penguins and other local species could be at risk after H5N1 was detected in the Antarctic region for the first time.\nThe virus was probably carried to Antarctica by migratory birds returning from South America, where there has been a high number of bird flu cases, according to the British Antarctic Survey.\nBird flu detected in Antarctica region for first time\nDhanasekaran said the H7 and H9 virus groups mainly infected chickens, while the biggest difficulty in controlling the H5 viruses was that they affected aquatic and terrestrial poultry differently.\n“When [H5] is infecting … poultry, it can kill chickens but it does not kill ducks. It is naturally adapted to ducks so when it is circulating in the live bird markets, we can never be sure until it infects chickens and they start dying,” he said.\nThe paper warned that the scale of H5 outbreaks in wild birds had been escalating beyond Asia since 2014. The researchers said recent incidences in African and European bird populations suggested the epicentre had also extended beyond the Asian region.\n“It is necessary to enhance global surveillance and improve multifaceted mitigation strategies for outbreak prevention and response,” the scientists said.\nBird flu outbreak: 500,000 chickens die in Burkina Faso\nThe World Health Organization recorded 1,566 cases of human infection and at least 613 deaths from the H7N9 virus between 2013 and 2018. China remained the epicentre of the virus until 2016.\nA 2021 study by the State Key Laboratory of Veterinary Biotechnology in Harbin, northern China, identified five waves of human infection caused by the H7N9 bird flu virus, and confirmed that it caused only mild infections in ducks while being highly lethal to chickens.\nThe Harbin researchers noted that “further human cases have been successfully prevented since September 2017 through the use of an H7N9 vaccine in poultry” but the virus had not been eradicated from poultry.\nThey found that the H7N9 viruses isolated in 2019 were antigenically different from the vaccine strain used to control the virus in poultry. “Replication of these viruses cannot therefore be completely prevented in vaccinated chickens.”\n\n\nMeanwhile in Britain, scientists proposed gene editing as a possible way to breed chickens that are partially resistant to bird flu, in a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature Communications in October.\nThe team said it was able to restrict the virus from infecting chickens by altering a small section of their DNA. The researchers found fully grown chickens were resistant to a very low dose of the flu from infected birds, but not at doses 1,000 times higher.\nCo-author Mike McGrew, personal chair of avian reproductive technologies at the University of Edinburgh’s Roslin Institute, said gene editing “promises a new way to make permanent changes in the disease resistance of an animal”.\n“This can be passed down through all the gene-edited animals to all the offspring so it would protect the poultry and reduce risk to the poultry farmers and wild birds,” he said.\nBird flu kills 11-year-old girl in Cambodia\nWendy Barclay, head of the department of infectious diseases at Imperial College London and the study’s other author, said her group discovered a gene called ANP32 which was “absolutely essential” to supporting the virus when it was inside the cell.\n“If you could prevent the protein from being used [to help viruses replicate] by gene editing, the virus would not be able to replicate. This strategy could be used not just for H5N1 bird flu, but for any of the strains, because it is fundamental to the way that the virus works,” she said.\nThe British scientists monitored the gene-edited birds for more than two years and said they showed no adverse effects on health or egg-laying productivity.\nThey cautioned that further study would be needed to ensure animal health, and that multiple edits of the gene family might be needed to eliminate the possibility of viral evolution.\n","json":[{"type":"track-viewed-percentage","attribs":{"percentage":"0"}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The deadly virus behind for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds in ‘environmental disaster’, International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds.\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"Epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds in ‘environmental disaster’, International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds."}]}]},"subHeadline":{"text":"International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds\nWith infections recorded across 5 continents, including Antarctica, penguins and marine and land-based mammals are ‘at risk’\n","json":[{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"International research team led by University of Hong Kong finds epicentre for deadly H5N1 virus has shifted, carried across the world by wild birds"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"With infections recorded across 5 continents, including Antarctica, penguins and marine and land-based mammals are ‘at risk’"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"}]}]},"body":{"text":"The deadly virus behind bird flu has become better adapted to wild birds and is shifting from China to Europe and northern Africa, in what a study led by the University of Hong Kong (HKU) has called “an environmental disaster”.\nThe team, which included researchers in Australia, Egypt, France, the United Arab Emirates and the United States, found that recent outbreaks suggested the epicentre for the H5N1 virus had extended beyond Asia.\nIn a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature, the researchers said the virus had also become more persistent in wild bird populations, driving the evolution and spread of new strains, putting marine and land mammals at risk across the world.\n“Since November 2021, this H5N1 virus has caused unprecedented outbreaks in diverse wild bird species across five continents and a significant rise in incidental infections in wild carnivores, mink farms and marine mammals,” they said.\nLead author Vijaykrishna Dhanasekaran, who heads the HKU pathogen evolution lab, urged China to guard against strains of the virus arriving from Europe by keeping up its vaccination programme against bird flu infections among the country’s flocks.\nWhy sick minks are reigniting worries about bird flu spreading to humans\n“Especially since the emergence of H7N9 in 2013 … the control in China has become really good with mass application of H5 and H7 vaccines. That is one of the reasons the recent resurgence was not in China,” he said.\n“They need to sustain it because now the viruses are coming back from Europe. [China should] continue the vaccination system so that we can keep eliminating the virus.”\nH5N1 first emerged in China in 1996 and was the first virus to establish sustained transmission in domestic poultry. After years of being largely confined to Asian poultry networks, recent outbreaks have emerged further afield.\nOn the rare occasions the virus appeared in Europe and Africa, it was carried there by wild birds but would die out after a few months because H5N1 was not as adaptive to wild populations.\n“However, in the past two years we have seen extensive outbreaks – they’re doubling,” said Dhanasekaran, who is also an associate professor with the HKU school of public health.\n“It’s an environmental disaster in terms of the number of wild birds that have been infected. The virus is also spreading to new regions via these migratory flyways.”\n‘Extremely rare’ case of bird flu in human detected in England\nIn September, three adult harbour seals in Puget Sound, on the northwestern coast of Washington state, tested positive for the H5N1 virus, according to the US National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration.\nIt was the first time the highly pathogenic bird flu was reported in marine mammals on the US West Coast.\nLast month, concerns were raised that penguins and other local species could be at risk after H5N1 was detected in the Antarctic region for the first time.\nThe virus was probably carried to Antarctica by migratory birds returning from South America, where there has been a high number of bird flu cases, according to the British Antarctic Survey.\nBird flu detected in Antarctica region for first time\nDhanasekaran said the H7 and H9 virus groups mainly infected chickens, while the biggest difficulty in controlling the H5 viruses was that they affected aquatic and terrestrial poultry differently.\n“When [H5] is infecting … poultry, it can kill chickens but it does not kill ducks. It is naturally adapted to ducks so when it is circulating in the live bird markets, we can never be sure until it infects chickens and they start dying,” he said.\nThe paper warned that the scale of H5 outbreaks in wild birds had been escalating beyond Asia since 2014. The researchers said recent incidences in African and European bird populations suggested the epicentre had also extended beyond the Asian region.\n“It is necessary to enhance global surveillance and improve multifaceted mitigation strategies for outbreak prevention and response,” the scientists said.\nBird flu outbreak: 500,000 chickens die in Burkina Faso\nThe World Health Organization recorded 1,566 cases of human infection and at least 613 deaths from the H7N9 virus between 2013 and 2018. China remained the epicentre of the virus until 2016.\nA 2021 study by the State Key Laboratory of Veterinary Biotechnology in Harbin, northern China, identified five waves of human infection caused by the H7N9 bird flu virus, and confirmed that it caused only mild infections in ducks while being highly lethal to chickens.\nThe Harbin researchers noted that “further human cases have been successfully prevented since September 2017 through the use of an H7N9 vaccine in poultry” but the virus had not been eradicated from poultry.\nThey found that the H7N9 viruses isolated in 2019 were antigenically different from the vaccine strain used to control the virus in poultry. “Replication of these viruses cannot therefore be completely prevented in vaccinated chickens.”\n\n\nMeanwhile in Britain, scientists proposed gene editing as a possible way to breed chickens that are partially resistant to bird flu, in a paper published by the peer-reviewed journal Nature Communications in October.\nThe team said it was able to restrict the virus from infecting chickens by altering a small section of their DNA. The researchers found fully grown chickens were resistant to a very low dose of the flu from infected birds, but not at doses 1,000 times higher.\nCo-author Mike McGrew, personal chair of avian reproductive technologies at the University of Edinburgh’s Roslin Institute, said gene editing “promises a new way to make permanent changes in the disease resistance of an animal”.\n“This can be passed down through all the gene-edited animals to all the offspring so it would protect the poultry and reduce risk to the poultry farmers and wild birds,” he said.\nBird flu kills 11-year-old girl in Cambodia\nWendy Barclay, head of the department of infectious diseases at Imperial College London and the study’s other author, said her group discovered a gene called ANP32 which was “absolutely essential” to supporting the virus when it was inside the cell.\n“If you could prevent the protein from being used [to help viruses replicate] by gene editing, the virus would not be able to replicate. This strategy could be used not just for H5N1 bird flu, but for any of the strains, because it is fundamental to the way that the virus works,” she said.\nThe British scientists monitored the gene-edited birds for more than two years and said they showed no adverse effects on health or egg-laying productivity.\nThey cautioned that further study would be needed to ensure animal health, and that multiple edits of the gene family might be needed to eliminate the possibility of viral evolution.\n","json":[{"type":"track-viewed-percentage","attribs":{"percentage":"0"}},{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The deadly virus behind
